reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    A model that performs a 3D transposed convolution, followed by batch normalization, 
    two average pooling layers.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, bias_shape):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding)
        self.batch_norm = nn.BatchNorm3d(out_channels)
        self.avg_pool1 = nn.AvgPool3d(kernel_size=2)
        self.avg_pool2 = nn.AvgPool3d(kernel_size=2)

    def forward(self, x):
        x = self.conv_transpose(x)
        x = self.batch_norm(x)
        x = self.avg_pool1(x)
        x = self.avg_pool2(x)
        return x


batch_size = 64
in_channels = 3
out_channels = 16
depth, height, width = 32, 32, 32
kernel_size = 3
stride = 2
padding = 1
bias_shape = (out_channels, 1, 1, 1)

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, depth, height, width)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, bias_shape]

reasoning
We need to write a custom CUDA kernel that replaces the forward computation of `Model` (ConvTranspose3d -> BatchNorm3d -> AvgPool3d -> AvgPool3d) and run faster than the PyTorch reference. First, let's analyze the operations and determine the expected output sizes and what fusions are possible. Given: - Input x: (batch_size=64, in_channels=3, D=32, H=32, W=32) - ConvTranspose3d: in_channels=3, out_channels=16, kernel_size=3, stride=2, padding=1 - BatchNorm3d: out_channels=16 - AvgPool3d(kernel_size=2) - AvgPool3d(kernel_size=2) Let's compute the output shape after each operation. **ConvTranspose3d output size formula:** For each dimension: output_size = (input_size - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + kernel_size + output_padding Default output_padding = 0. So for D: (32 - 1) * 2 - 2 * 1 + 3 = 31*2 - 2 + 3 = 62 - 2 + 3 = 63. Output: (64, 16, 63, 63, 63) **BatchNorm3d:** same shape: (64, 16, 63, 63, 63) **AvgPool3d(kernel_size=2):** floor division. 63 // 2 = 31. After first pool: (64, 16, 31, 31, 31) **AvgPool3d(kernel_size=2):** again floor division. 31 // 2 = 15. After second pool: (64, 16, 15, 15, 15) Wait, 63//2 = 31 (since integer division discards remainder). Yes. 31//2 = 15. Final output: (64, 16, 15, 15, 15) Now we need to write a fused CUDA kernel that performs all these operations in one go. Key observations: 1. Two consecutive AvgPool3d(kernel_size=2) is equivalent to one AvgPool3d(kernel_size=4) with stride 4? Wait, no. AvgPool3d is applied sequentially with kernel_size=2 and stride=2 (default stride = kernel_size for AvgPool3d when stride is not specified). Yes, default stride is kernel_size. So first pool: kernel=2, stride=2. Second pool: kernel=2, stride=2. The composition of two average pooling layers with kernel=2, stride=2 each is equivalent to a single average pooling with kernel=4, stride=4? Actually, it is equivalent to a kernel=4 pooling with stride=4 but with a specific weighting. Let's verify. For 1D case: First avg pool kernel=2, stride=2: output[i] = (x[2i] + x[2i+1]) / 2 Second avg pool kernel=2, stride=2: output[j] = (out[2j] + out[2j+1]) / 2 = ((x[4j] + x[4j+1])/2 + (x[4j+2] + x[4j+3])/2) / 2 = (x[4j] + x[4j+1] + x[4j+2] + x[4j+3]) / 4 So yes, sequential avg pool with kernel=2, stride=2 twice is exactly a single avg pool with kernel=4, stride=4, in 1D. In 3D, because each dimension is independent, the 3D average over a 2x2x2 cube twice is equivalent to a 4x4x4 cube average (with stride 4). The effective receptive field is 4x4x4 and the normalization factor is 64 = 4*4*4. Let's check the output size: After first pool: 63//2 = 31 After second pool: 31//2 = 15 A single kernel=4, stride=4 pool on 63: floor(63/4) = 15. Yes. So we can fuse the two avg pools into a single pooling layer that averages over a 4x4x4 region with stride 4. However, we can go even further: fuse the entire sequence: ConvTranspose3d + BatchNorm + AvgPool(4x4x4 stride 4). But ConvTranspose3d is expensive and produces intermediate data of size (64, 16, 63, 63, 63) which is about 64 * 16 * 63^3 * 4 bytes ≈ 64 * 16 * 250047 * 4 bytes. Wait 63^3 = 250047? Let's calculate: 63^2 = 3969, 3969*63 = 250047. So total elements = 64 * 16 * 250047 = 256 * 250047 = about 64 million elements. At fp32, that's ~256 MB. That's a lot. So fusion is highly beneficial to save memory bandwidth. We need to write a CUDA kernel that computes for each output element (b, c, d_out, h_out, w_out) the average of the 4x4x4 block of the conv transpose + batch norm output, but we can compute the value for each element in the 4x4x4 block on the fly and accumulate. Wait, but ConvTranspose3d also accepts a bias. In the PyTorch reference, `nn.ConvTranspose3d` has a bias by default. However, the `bias_shape` argument is passed to the constructor but not used in the reference? Let's look at the `__init__` signature: `(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, bias_shape)`. The reference sets `self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose3d(...)` which will initialize its own bias parameter. So the `bias_shape` argument is actually unused in the reference model. That's weird but we must replicate it. Wait, `bias_shape` is `(out_channels, 1, 1, 1)`. The reference doesn't use it. Does the reference have a bias in conv_transpose? Yes, PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d has bias=True by default. So the model output depends on the conv_transpose's bias. Our custom kernel must replicate that. Since we are replacing the forward computation, we need to access the weights and bias from the PyTorch module, or we can simply compute using the PyTorch tensors from `self.conv_transpose.weight`, `self.conv_transpose.bias`, and `self.batch_norm` parameters (running_mean, running_var, weight, bias, eps). But the `ModelNew` class must accept the same constructor arguments as `Model`. We can instantiate the same submodules inside `ModelNew.__init__` and then in forward use their parameters in our custom kernel. That's allowed because the constructor arguments are the same and the forward signature is the same. This is a common approach: keep the reference parameters but compute with a custom kernel. However, there's a subtlety: the `Model` uses `nn.ConvTranspose3d` which in its `__init__` initializes weights and bias. We can instantiate it in `ModelNew` as well, and then in forward, read its parameters and pass them to the CUDA kernel. But `nn.ConvTranspose3d` also requires the same constructor arguments, which we have. Similarly for BatchNorm3d, we need it to initialize running_mean, running_var, weight, bias. So `ModelNew.__init__` can simply call `super().__init__()` and instantiate `self.conv_transpose` and `self.batch_norm` and `self.avg_pool1`, `self.avg_pool2` just like `Model`. Then in forward, we can read the parameters and call our fused kernel. But is it necessary to instantiate the pooling layers? No, we can drop them and just keep the parameters. But to be safe, we can instantiate them, but they won't be used in forward. Actually, keeping them is fine. Alternatively, we can just use `nn.ConvTranspose3d` and `nn.BatchNorm3d` in `__init__` to get their parameters, and not instantiate the pooling layers at all. But the same constructor arguments mean we can ignore any unused ones. The key is to write a fused kernel. Let's analyze the ConvTranspose3d operation. Input x: (N, Cin, D_in, H_in, W_in) = (64, 3, 32, 32, 32) Weight w: (Cin, Cout, K, K, K) = (3, 16, 3, 3, 3) NOTE: For ConvTranspose3d, the weight shape is (in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size[0], kernel_size[1], kernel_size[2]). Actually in PyTorch, ConvTranspose3d weight is (in_channels, out_channels/groups, kD, kH, kW). Wait, check PyTorch docs: For ConvTranspose3d, weight shape is (in_channels, out_channels/groups, kernel_size[0], kernel_size[1], kernel_size[2]). Since groups=1, it's (in_channels, out_channels, kD, kH, kW). Yes. Bias: (out_channels,) BatchNorm parameters: weight (gamma): (out_channels,) bias (beta): (out_channels,) running_mean: (out_channels,) running_var: (out_channels,) eps: default 1e-5 Output after BN and pooling: (N, Cout, D_out, H_out, W_out) where D_out = H_out = W_out = 15. For each output position (n, c, d, h, w) in the final output, the value is the average over the 4x4x4 block of the conv-transpose+bn output at positions: d' in [4d, 4d+3] h' in [4h, 4h+3] w' in [4w, 4w+3] But wait, the effective output of conv_transpose is size 63. So the max index for d' is 4*14+3 = 59. And 63 is not divisible by 4. So the last few elements (60,61,62) are not included in the pooling. That's fine because avg pool with kernel=2 stride=2 twice ignores the remainders. Actually first pool on 63 -> 31, second pool on 31 -> 15. The indices covered are: After first pool: output indices correspond to input ranges: 0: [0,1] 1: [2,3] ... 30: [60,61] Input index 62 is dropped. After second pool on 31 -> 15: 0: [0,1] of first pool output -> input [0,1,2,3] ... 14: [28,29] of first pool output -> input [56,57,58,59] So input indices 60,61,62 are dropped. So the effective block is indeed [0,59] for each dimension. And 59 = 4*14 + 3. So the mapping is correct: for output (d,h,w) we average over [4d, 4d+3] in each dimension, but only for d,h,w from 0 to 14. Since floor(63/4)=15. The value at each intermediate position (d', h', w') before pooling is: y_intermediate[n,c,d',h',w'] = (conv_transpose_output[n,c,d',h',w'] - running_mean[c]) / sqrt(running_var[c] + eps) * gamma[c] + beta[c] And conv_transpose_output is computed as the sum over input channels and kernel offsets of: x[n, cin, d_in, h_in, w_in] * weight[cin, c, kd, kh, kw] + bias[c] But ConvTranspose3d with stride=2, padding=1, output_padding=0. The formula for transposed convolution (also called fractionally-strided convolution) is that each input element contributes to a neighborhood in the output determined by the kernel. Specifically, for output index (d', h', w'): d' = (d_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + kd with d_in ranging over valid inputs such that d' is in [0, output_size-1], and kd in [0, K-1]. Alternatively, the standard way to compute transposed conv is: For each input position (n, cin, din, hin, win), we add `x[n, cin, din, hin, win] * weight[cin, cout, :, :, :]` to the output region: d_start = din * stride - padding Then for each kd in [0, K-1], the affected output d' is d_start + kd, provided d' is in [0, D_out-1]. Wait, there are multiple equivalent ways. In PyTorch, ConvTranspose3d is implemented as the gradient of Conv3d. The forward of ConvTranspose3d corresponds to the backward of Conv3d. The formula for output value at (n, cout, d', h', w') is: sum_{cin, kd, kh, kw} x[n, cin, d_in, h_in, w_in] * weight[cin, cout, kd, kh, kw] + bias[cout] where d_in = floor((d' + padding - kd) / stride) ??? No. Let's derive it carefully. Conv3d forward: output[n, cout, d, h, w] = sum_{cin, kd, kh, kw} x[n, cin, d*stride - padding + kd, h*stride - padding + kh, w*stride - padding + kw] * weight[cout, cin, kd, kh, kw] + bias[cout] (assuming stride and padding). The backward w.r.t. input of Conv3d (which is ConvTranspose3d forward) is: For each output position (n, cout, d', h', w'), we have: grad_input[n, cin, di, hi, wi] = sum_{cout, kd, kh, kw} grad_output[n, cout, d', h', w'] * weight[cout, cin, kd, kh, kw] where d' = di*stride - padding + kd. Wait, that's the forward of conv transpose but with the weight transposed. Actually in PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d, the weight is stored as (in_channels, out_channels, kD, kH, kW). The operation corresponds to: output[n, :, d', h', w'] = sum_{cin, kd, kh, kw} x[n, cin, d_in, h_in, w_in] * weight[cin, :, kd, kh, kw] + bias where the relationship between (d_in, kd) and d' is the same as in conv3d: d' = d_in * stride - padding + kd. We need to invert this to express the output as a sum over input elements. For a fixed output position (d', h', w'), which input positions contribute? d_in must satisfy: d' = d_in * stride - padding + kd for some kd in [0, K-1]. => d_in = (d' + padding - kd) / stride. Since d_in must be integer, and stride=2, only certain kd work. Specifically, for each kd such that (d' + padding - kd) is divisible by stride and d_in is in range. With stride=2, padding=1: d_in = (d' + 1 - kd) / 2. For kd=0: d_in = (d'+1)/2. Integer when d' is odd. For kd=1: d_in = d'/2. Integer when d' is even. For kd=2: d_in = (d'-1)/2. Integer when d' is odd. Wait, let's check small example. Input size=2, stride=2, padding=1, kernel=3. Output size = (2-1)*2 - 2*1 + 3 = 2 - 2 + 3 = 3. Output indices: 0,1,2. For d'=0: kd=0: d_in = (0+1)/2 = 0.5 no. kd=1: d_in = (0)/2 = 0. yes. kd=2: d_in = (-1)/2 = -0.5 no. So only kd=1, d_in=0. For d'=1: kd=0: d_in = (2)/2 =1. yes. kd=1: d_in = (1)/2=0.5 no. kd=2: d_in = (0)/2=0. yes. So kd=0,d_in=1 and kd=2,d_in=0. For d'=2: kd=0: d_in = 3/2=1.5 no. kd=1: d_in=2/2=1. yes. kd=2: d_in=1/2=0.5 no. So kd=1,d_in=1. This indeed matches the "dilated" pattern of transposed convolution. So the naive approach to compute the output at each (d',h',w') by iterating over input and kernel is a bit complicated because the mapping depends on parity. However, we can think of it differently: transposed convolution with stride S is equivalent to inserting S-1 zeros between input elements, then applying a normal convolution with stride 1 and appropriate padding. Specifically, for stride=2, padding=1: We can create a dilated input of size (2*D_in - 1) where we interleave zeros. Then apply a conv3d with stride=1, padding=1? Let's verify. Input size = 32. Dilated size = 32*2 -1 = 63. Conv3d with kernel=3, stride=1, padding=1 on size 63 gives output size = 63 - 3 + 2*1 + 1? Wait, formula for conv: output = (input + 2*padding - kernel) / stride + 1 = (63 + 2*1 - 3)/1 + 1 = 63. Yes. So this matches the output size. The weight for this equivalent conv3d would be the same as the transposed conv weight but transposed? Wait. In PyTorch, `nn.ConvTranspose3d` with weight W (shape Cin x Cout x K x K x K) essentially performs a conv3d on the dilated input with weight W' where W'[cout, cin, ...] = W[cin, cout, ...]? Let's check the gradient formulation. Actually, the gradient of Conv3d w.r.t. input uses the weight rotated by 180 degrees (or flipped). For a 1D conv, grad_input = conv_transpose(grad_output, weight_flipped, stride=stride, padding=padding). The weight used in transposed conv is the same as the weight of the conv, not flipped, because PyTorch's ConvTranspose already handles the appropriate indexing internally. In other words, `nn.ConvTranspose3d` is mathematically equivalent to a conv3d on dilated input with the same weight but with input/output channel dimensions swapped? Let's verify with a known fact: In PyTorch, `nn.ConvTranspose2d` weight shape is (in_channels, out_channels, kH, kW). It computes the gradient of conv2d. The operation can be seen as conv2d on the dilated input with the weight transposed and rotated. But when we access `conv_transpose.weight`, it is the weight tensor as stored: shape (in_channels, out_channels, ...). In the forward pass, it is used directly without explicit transposition or flipping inside our kernel? We need to match PyTorch's numerical output exactly. The simplest way to guarantee correctness is to let PyTorch compute the conv_transpose in a reference way during initialization and then copy the weight into our kernel with the exact same usage pattern. But we need to know the exact indexing. Alternatively, we can avoid writing the conv_transpose from scratch by using `torch.nn.functional.conv_transpose3d` inside the kernel (but that would require launching separate kernels, defeating fusion). We need to fuse. One approach: compute the conv_transpose output on the fly for each intermediate element, but this is complicated due to the sparse access pattern. However, we can precompute the "dilated" input in shared memory or global memory? That would increase memory usage. Another approach: Instead of fusing the conv_transpose, we could compute conv_transpose using PyTorch, then fuse BN and the two avg pools into a single kernel. The intermediate tensor after conv_transpose is ~256MB. BN + avg pool on a 256MB tensor twice might be bandwidth-bound. Fusing BN and the two pools would save bandwidth. But conv_transpose is compute-bound and takes significant time. If we compute conv_transpose with PyTorch, we have to write that 256MB to global memory. Then our fused kernel reads it once. The total time might still be dominated by conv_transpose compute. But maybe we can still beat the reference by fusing the rest. Let's check the reference time. Let me analyze the reference model: 1. `ConvTranspose3d` from `in_channels=3` to `out_channels=16`, `kernel_size=3`, `stride=2`, `padding=1` 2. `BatchNorm3d` with `out_channels=16` 3. `AvgPool3d(kernel_size=2)` - first pooling 4. `AvgPool3d(kernel_size=2)` - second pooling Input shape: `[64, 3, 32, 32, 32]` Let me compute the output shapes: - ConvTranspose3d with stride=2, padding=1, kernel=3: - Output D/H/W = (32 - 1) * 2 - 2*1 + 3 = 62 - 2 + 3 = 63 ... wait let me recalculate. - For ConvTranspose3d: output_size = (input_size - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + kernel_size + output_padding - Default output_padding = 0 - Output = (32 - 1) * 2 - 2 * 1 + 3 = 62 - 2 + 3 = 63 Actually let me verify: `L_out = (L_in - 1) × stride - 2 × padding + kernel_size + output_padding` For our case: `(32 - 1) * 2 - 2*1 + 3 = 62 - 2 + 3 = 63` So conv_transpose output is `[64, 16, 63, 63, 63]` Then BatchNorm3d preserves shape. First AvgPool3d(2): 63 // 2 = 31, output `[64, 16, 31, 31, 31]` Second AvgPool3d(2): 31 // 2 = 15, output `[64, 16, 15, 15, 15]` Now, I need to fuse these operations into a single CUDA kernel. The main operations are: 1. ConvTranspose3d: Kind of like a "reverse" convolution - each input element contributes to multiple output elements via the kernel weights. Or equivalently, it's like a regular convolution with dilated input. 2. BatchNorm: `(x - mean) * gamma / sqrt(var + eps) + beta` 3. Two average poolings: each 2x2x2 averaging For a fused kernel, the most efficient approach would be to: 1. For each output pixel after both poolings 2. Compute which region in the intermediate tensors it corresponds to 3. Trace back through the operations However, tracing through two avg pools and then conv_transpose can be complex. Let me think about this differently. Actually, the most practical approach is to fuse the conv_transpose and batch_norm into one operation (which is standard), but also try to fuse the two avg_pools. Since the output after both pools is relatively small `[64, 16, 15, 15, 15]` (only 3,456,000 elements), we could compute each output element by iterating over the relevant input region. For a given output position after two avg pools: - After pool2: position (n, c, d, h, w) - This averages the 2x2x2 region in the output of pool1: d*2:(d+1)*2, h*2:(h+1)*2, w*2:(w+1)*2 - After pool1: position (n, c, d2, h2, w2) - This averages the 2x2x2 region in the output of BN (which equals conv_transpose output): d2*2:(d2+1)*2, etc. So the effective receptive field after both pools is 4x4x4 in the conv_transpose output space. For each element in this 4x4x4 region, we need to trace back through the conv_transpose. Wait, actually a simpler approach: perform the conv_transpose to get intermediate output, then do the two avg pools. But since the conv_transpose output is large `[64, 16, 63, 63, 63]`, we need to make it efficient. Total conv_transpose output: 64 * 16 * 63^3 = 64 * 16 * 250047 = 256,048,128 elements ≈ 256M elements. Hmm, that's quite large for fp32 (1 GB memory). Actually, let me think about whether we can fuse everything more directly. For ConvTranspose3d with stride=2, each output pixel gets contributions from multiple input pixels. Specifically, with stride=2 and kernel=3, each output coordinate maps to input coordinates in a specific pattern. But with two subsequent avg pools, each output pixel is the average of a 4x4x4 region in the conv_transpose output. So for each output pixel (after both pools), we could iterate over the 4x4x4 region in conv_transpose output space, and for each position in that region, compute which input pixels contribute to it, apply the kernel weights and BN parameters, and accumulate. But each conv_transpose output pixel gets contributions from at most C_in * something input pixels. Actually for transposed conv with stride 2, kernel 3, each output pixel gets contributions from at most 4 input pixels per input channel (because the kernel is 3x3x3, and with dilation due to stride, the effective neighborhood is small). Wait, let me think more carefully. In a regular conv with stride 2, each output pixel depends on a 3x3x3 region of input, strided. In a transposed conv with stride 2, each input pixel "spreads out" to a 3x3x3 region of output, strided. Actually for ConvTranspose3d with stride 2, it's equivalent to putting zeros between input elements (dilation) and then doing a regular conv. More precisely, if we insert stride-1 zeros between input elements, then convolve with kernel_size=3. So each input element contributes to at most 3x3x3 = 27 output elements, but because of the zero insertion, the contribution pattern is spread out. For our output after pools, each final output element depends on a 4x4x4 region in conv_transpose output. For each of those 64 positions, we need to know which input elements contribute. After accumulating, divide by pool size, apply BN, write output Wait, step 4 is tricky. I said we need the average of BN(conv_out) over the 4x4x4 region. But we can compute BN(conv_out) for each position in the region and average, OR first average conv_out and then apply BN. Is averaging then BN the same as BN then averaging? No! BN(conv_out) = gamma * (conv_out - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) + beta Average(BN(conv_out)) = gamma * (avg(conv_out) - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) + beta Because mean and gamma are per-channel constants, and average is linear: avg(a*x + b) = a*avg(x) + b So YES! We can compute the average of conv_out first, then apply BN! This is a huge simplification. We don't need to apply BN to each of the 64 positions. We just: 1. Compute the average of conv_out over the 4x4x4 region (call it pool_val) 2. Then compute BN(pool_val) = gamma * (pool_val - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) + beta Wait, is that correct? avg(BN(conv_out)) = avg(gamma * (conv_out - mean) / std + beta) = gamma / std * avg(conv_out - mean) + beta = gamma / std * (avg(conv_out) - mean) + beta = BN(avg(conv_out)) YES! Perfect! So the algorithm simplifies to: For each final output element (n, c, d2, h2, w2): 1. Compute avg(conv_out) over the corresponding region 2. Apply BN to this average Where conv_out is the conv_transpose output (before BN). Now, avg(conv_out) over the region = sum(conv_out in region) / count. So we just need to compute the sum of conv_out over the 4×4×4 region, then apply BN. And conv_out at a point = sum over in_c, kd, kh, kw of input[in_c, id, ih, iw] * weight[c, in_c, kd, kh, kw]. By linearity: sum(conv_out in region) = sum over pos in region, in_c, kd, kh, kw of input[in_c, id(pos, kd), ih(pos, kh), iw(pos, kw)] * weight[c, in_c, kd, kh, kw] We can swap the order: = sum over in_c, kd, kh, kw of weight[c, in_c, kd, kh, kw] * sum over pos in region of input[in_c, id(pos, kd), ih(pos, kh), iw(pos, kw)] For a fixed kernel offset (kd, kh, kw), the inner sum is over pos in the 4×4×4 region, where each pos gives a conv_out point at (d, h, w), and we look up input at id = (d + 1 - kd)/2 (when valid). So for fixed kd, kh, kw, we're summing input values over a transformed version of the 4×4×4 region. Plan for fully fused kernel: - Launch one thread per final output element (3.4M threads) - Each thread computes the sum of conv_transpose values over its pool region - Then applies BN Grid/block: - Block size: 256 threads - Grid: enough to cover 64 * 16 * 15 * 15 * 15 = 3,456,000 elements - 3,456,000 / 256 = 13,500 blocks Each thread: ```c int n = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; // or some mapping // Actually better to map linear index to (n, c, d2, h2, w2) // Determine pool region bounds int d_start = d2 * 4; int d_end = min(d_start + 4, 63); // similarly for h, w // For d2=14: d_start=56, d_end=63 (7 values) float sum = 0; for (int d = d_start; d < d_end; d++) { for (int h = h_start; h < h_end; h++) { for (int w = w_start; w < w_end; w++) { // Compute conv_transpose at (n, c, d, h, w) float conv_val = 0; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < 3; c_in++) { for (int kd = 0; kd < 3; kd++) { int d_offset = d + 1 - kd; if (d_offset % 2 != 0) continue; int id = d_offset / 2; if (id < 0 || id >= 32) continue; // similarly for h, w for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { int h_offset = h + 1 - kh; if (h_offset % 2 != 0) continue; int ih = h_offset / 2; if (ih < 0 || ih >= 32) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int w_offset = w + 1 - kw; if (w_offset % 2 != 0) continue; int iw = w_offset / 2; if (iw < 0 || iw >= 32) continue; float in_val = input[n, c_in, id, ih, iw]; float w_val = weight[c, c_in, kd, kh, kw]; conv_val += in_val * w_val; } } } } sum += conv_val; } } } float avg = sum / ((d_end - d_start) * (h_end - h_start) * (w_end - w_start)); float out_val = gamma[c] * (avg - mean[c]) / sqrt(var[c] + eps) + beta[c]; output[n, c, d2, h2, w2] = out_val; ``` This is straightforward but has a lot of branching. Let me optimize the branching. For the inner loops, the `if (offset % 2 != 0) continue` checks can be simplified. For a given d, the valid kd values are: - If d is even: kd = 1 only (since d+1 is odd, kd must be odd, and kd=1 is the only odd in {0,1,2} that gives valid id) - If d is odd: kd ∈ {0, 2} (since d+1 is even, kd must be even) Wait, I computed this earlier. Let me verify: BN(x) = gamma * (x - mean) / std + beta avg(BN(conv + bias)) over region R = avg(gamma * (conv + bias - mean) / std + beta) = gamma / std * avg(conv + bias - mean) + beta = gamma / std * (avg(conv) + bias - mean) + beta = gamma * (avg(conv) + bias - mean) / std + beta = BN(avg(conv) + bias) Yes! reasoning
Let me debug the issue. The compilation failed because `fused_forward` was not found in the cpp_sources scope when generating the pybind11 binding. The issue seems to be that the function `fused_forward` was only declared in the cuda source but its definition needs to be visible to the pybind11 wrapper. Actually, looking at the `load_inline` API, I need to: 1. Put the function declaration in the C++ source (cpp_sources) so pybind11 can see it 2. Put the implementation in cuda_sources Wait, but the pybind11 auto-generated wrapper is in main.cpp which includes the cuda code. Let me look at how `load_inline` actually works. When I use `load_inline` with `functions` argument, it generates a pybind11 module that calls `wrap_pybind_function` on each function in the `functions` list. For this to compile, each function must be declared at namespace scope in the C++ translation unit. Actually in many PyTorch examples, when using `load_inline`: - The CUDA implementation goes in `cuda_sources` - To expose it via pybind11, there should be a host function that wraps the kernel The typical pattern is: 1. Put everything in `cuda_sources` (including the `torch::Tensor` wrapper function) which contains both the kernel and the host function 2. In `cpp_sources`, include the header declarations or use `extern` declarations Wait, but I did step 1. The issue is that `load_inline` generates a C++ file that tries to call `torch::wrap_pybind_function(fused_forward)`, but since the CUDA code was compiled as a separate translation unit (cuda.cu), the C++ compiler doesn't see the declaration. One solution is to provide a declaration in `cpp_sources`: ```cpp torch::Tensor fused_forward(torch::Tensor input, ...); ``` Or I can use `extern "C"` but that's not necessary. Let me provide a declaration in cpp_sources and the implementation in cuda_sources.

reasoning
The correctness test passes with zero error, which is excellent. Now let me think about whether this kernel is actually faster than the reference. The reference workload: 1. ConvTranspose3d: (64, 3, 32, 32, 32) -> (64, 16, 63, 63, 63) with stride=2 2. BatchNorm: on (64, 16, 63, 63, 63) 3. AvgPool3d(2): (64, 16, 63, 63, 63) -> (64, 16, 31, 31, 31) -- Wait, 63/2 = 31 (integer division) 4. AvgPool3d(2): (64, 16, 31, 31, 31) -> (64, 16, 15, 15, 15) So the output shape is (64, 16, 15, 15, 15), which matches my kernel. My fused kernel computes each output element independently. Each output at (n, c, d2, h2, w2) requires summing contributions from a 4×4×4 region of the conv_transpose output (each corner of a 4×4×4 block from 4×4×4 = 64 values), then dividing by 64, applying bias, and BN. The computation per thread is quite significant (up to 8 input reads × 8 weight reads = up to 64 multiply-adds per inner loop). But wait, let me recount: For each of the 64 values in the 4×4×4 conv_transpose region: - Each value at (d, h, w) (where d = 4d2..4d2+3, etc.) maps to up to 8 input values - For each input channel (3) and each valid kernel position Actually, I need to think about this more carefully. In my kernel: - For each of the 4×4×4 = 64 conv positions in the pool region - For each position, there are up to 8 contributions (from the 8 possible (id,ih,iw) combinations for odd coordinates, or 1 combination for even coordinates) - So there are up to 64 × 8 = 512 iterations of the innermost loop - Each inner loop does 3 multiplications (one per input channel) and 2 additions So each thread does up to 512 × 3 = 1536 FMA operations. This is actually quite a bit of register pressure and computation per thread. For 64 × 16 × 15³ = 3,240,000 output elements, with 1536 FMAs per element, that's about 5 billion FMAs total. At 67 TFLOPS, this should take about 0.075 ms if compute bound. But each thread also reads quite a lot of memory: - Input: each thread reads up to 8 × 3 = 24 float values from the input (but with locality, many threads in a block read overlapping values) - Weight: each thread reads 8 × 3 = 24 float values (but the same weight values are used across all threads for the same output channel) - Output: 1 write per thread The memory bandwidth for the input is the dominating factor. 64 batch * 3 channels * 32³ inputs = 6.29 MB of input data. For weights, it's 16 out_channels * 3 in_channels * 27 = ~5 KB. So input bandwidth is the bottleneck. But wait, the issue is that each warp of 32 threads may access very scattered parts of the input, leading to poor memory coalescing. Let me think about the memory access pattern: For output element (n, c, d2, h2, w2), the input accesses are at positions: - id = floor(d / 2) or floor(d / 2) - 1 (when d is odd) - ih = floor(h / 2) or floor(h / 2) - 1 (when h is odd) - iw = floor(w / 2) or floor(w / 2) - 1 (when w is odd) For threads in a warp processing consecutive linear indices: - Thread 0: (n0, c0, d20, h20, w20) - Thread 1: (n0, c0, d20, h20, w20+1) - ... Consecutive w2 values will access consecutive iw values (which is good for memory coalescing). But there will be strided accesses for different h2 values. Actually, for consecutive linear indices in the output, w2 increments first, then h2, then d2, then c, then n. So a warp of 32 threads will process 32 consecutive w2 values (modulo 15), which means mostly consecutive iw accesses. This should be reasonably coalesced for the w dimension, but there will be some non-coalescing when crossing h2 and d2 boundaries. Let me think about optimization. One issue with my current kernel is that the memory access pattern might not be ideal. Another issue is register pressure - with all those nested loops and temporaries, the compiler might use many registers, reducing occupancy. An alternative approach: Since the pooling is 2×2×2 with stride 2, and the conv_transpose has output size 63, the pooling regions are: - For 63 elements, the pooling with kernel=2, stride=2 creates output size floor((63 - 2) / 2) + 1 = 31. Wait, that's for the first pooling. Then floor((31 - 2) / 2) + 1 = 15. Actually, let me recalculate: AvgPool3d(kernel_size=2, stride=2) on 63: output = (63 - 2) // 2 + 1 = 61 // 2 + 1 = 30 + 1 = 31. Then on 31: output = (31 - 2) // 2 + 1 = 29 // 2 + 1 = 14 + 1 = 15. Yes, so output is 15×15×15. Wait, but is the overlap handled correctly in my kernel? Let me trace through: - For output position d2 in [0, 14], I process d in [4d2, 4d2+3] - d2=0: d in {0,1,2,3} → d<63, all valid - d2=14: d in {56,57,58,59} → d<63, all valid - But wait, d goes up to 62, so d2=14 should also include d=60, 61, 62? That's 4 values, but my code computes 4d2 to 4d2+3 = 56 to 59. What about 60, 61, 62? Hmm, let me think again. The first AvgPool3d outputs 31 elements. The second AvgPool3d outputs 15 elements. For the second pool, output d2'' covers input d2' in [2*d2'', 2*d2''+1], and each d2' covers d in [2*d2', 2*d2'+1]. So d2'' covers d in [4*d2'', 4*d2''+3]. That's correct! But d2' ranges from 0 to 30. For d2''=14: d2' in [28, 29]. d2'=28 covers d in [56, 57]. d2'=29 covers d in [58, 59]. So d ranges from 56 to 59. For d2''=15 (doesn't exist, max is 14): would be d2' in [30, 31], but d2'=31 doesn't exist. So the maximum d is 59, which is within 0..62? Wait, the conv transpose output size is 63, meaning indices 0..62. So 59 is fine. But what about d=60, 61, 62? Are those included in any pool? d=60: d2' = 30 (valid), which is in range for the first pool. d2' = 30 covers d in [60, 61]. These are averaged in the first pool to produce output at d2'=30. Then for the second pool, d2'=30 would be paired with d2'=31 for d2''=15, but d2'' max is 14, and d2'=30 is odd, so it's not included. Wait, let me re-examine. In AvgPool3d with kernel=2, stride=2, the output at position i covers input positions [2i, 2i+1]. One optimization idea: Since each output is the average of 64 conv_transpose values, and each conv_transpose value is computed from the input and weights, maybe I can precompute the "effective" kernel that maps input directly to output. For each output position (d2, h2, w2), I need to compute the average over a 4×4×4 region of conv_transpose output. The conv_transpose at position (d, h, w) is computed as: conv(d, h, w) = sum over (ic, kd, kh, kw) of input(floor(d/2)+offset_d, ...) * W(oc, ic, kd, kh, kw) The averaging then computes: output(d2, h2, w2) = (1/64) * sum over ld, lh, lw in [0..3] of conv(4d2+ld, 4h2+lh, 4w2+lw) This can be reorganized as: output = (1/64) * sum over (ic, kd, kh, kw) of W(...) * [sum over ld, lh, lw of input(...)] The key insight is that the sum over ld, lh, lw of the input values can be precomputed! Let me verify with the standard formula for ConvTranspose3d. For o=0 (even): (0 - k + 1) = 1 - k. For k=1: (1-1)/2 = 0, valid. For k=0: 1/2 = 0.5, invalid. For k=2: -1/2, invalid. So only k=1 works. For o=1 (odd): (1 - k + 1) = 2 - k. For k=0: 2/2=1, valid. For k=2: 0/2=0, valid. For k=1: 1/2=0.5, invalid. So my previous analysis was correct. Now, for the average of 64 conv_transpose values, I can reorganize the computation. The sum of products is the product of sums only when the terms factorize, which they do here! sum over (d,h,w) of input(id(d), ih(h), iw(w)) = [sum over d of input_id(d)] * [sum over h of input_ih(h)] * [sum over w of input_iw(w)] Is that right? No, because input(id, ih, iw) is a 3D array, not a product of three 1D arrays. Actually, what I mean is: for fixed kd, kh, kw, the set of (id, ih, iw) that appear in the sum forms a Cartesian product. So: sum_{d,h,w} input(id(d, kd), ih(h, kh), iw(w, kw)) = sum_{id in S_d(kd)} sum_{ih in S_h(kh)} sum_{iw in S_w(kw)} input(id, ih, iw) * count(id, kd) * count(ih, kh) * count(iw, kw) Where S_d(kd) is the set of input d indices for kernel kd, and count(id, kd) is the number of d values in the block that map to input id for kernel kd. Now, the total sum over ALL (kd, kh, kw) is: sum_{kd, kh, kw} W(c, ic, kd, kh, kw) * sum_{id, ih, iw} input(id, ih, iw) * count_d(id, kd) * count_h(ih, kh) * count_w(iw, kw) = sum_{id, ih, iw} input(id, ih, iw) * sum_{kd, kh, kw} W(c, ic, kd, kh, kw) * count_d(id, kd) * count_h(ih, kh) * count_w(iw, kw) = sum_{id, ih, iw} input(id, ih, iw) * W_eff(c, ic, id_offset, ih_offset, iw_offset) Where W_eff is an effective weight that combines the original weights with the count factors, and id_offset = id - 2d2, etc. So for each output position (d2, h2, w2), the effective kernel is a 3×3×3 convolution (but with some kernel positions having zero weight because of boundary effects). Actually, let me compute W_eff explicitly. The non-zero terms are: For kd=0: id_offset in {1, 2} with counts {1, 1} For kd=1: id_offset in {0, 1} with counts {2, 2} For kd=2: id_offset in {0, 1} with counts {1, 1} So non-zero combinations for (kd, id_offset): - (0, 1): count=1 - (0, 2): count=1 - (1, 0): count=2 - (1, 1): count=2 - (2, 0): count=1 - (2, 1): count=1 The overall factor is count_d * count_h * count_w. So W_eff(id_offset, ih_offset, iw_offset) is: sum over (kd, kh, kw) of W(kd, kh, kw) * count_d(id_offset, kd) * count_h(ih_offset, kh) * count_w(iw_offset, kw) And id_offset ranges from 0 to 2, ih_offset from 0 to 2, iw_offset from 0 to 2. So W_eff is a 3×3×3 effective kernel! And the sum over the block simplifies to a regular 3D convolution of the input with W_eff! Actually, this is just a tensor contraction. We can precompute W_eff at initialization. Let me think about this: - Precompute W_eff(c, ic, id, ih, iw) = sum_{kd,kh,kw} W_transpose(c, ic, kd, kh, kw) * count_d(id,kd) * count_h(ih,kh) * count_w(iw,kw) - Precompute bias_eff(c) = bias(c) - BN fusion: compute scale(c) = bn_weight(c) / sqrt(bn_var(c) + eps) - bias_eff(c) = (bias_eff(c) - bn_mean(c)) * scale(c) + bn_bias(c) - W_eff(c, ic, ...) = W_eff(c, ic, ...) * scale(c) / 64 Then forward is just: F.conv3d(input, W_eff, bias=bias_eff, stride=2) Wait, but I also need to divide by 64 (from the average pooling). Actually, the bias is added to each conv_out value first, then the 64 values are averaged. So: avg2 = (1/64) * sum_{64 values} (conv_value + bias) = (1/64) * sum_{64 values} conv_value + bias Because sum of 64 bias values = 64 * bias, divided by 64 = bias. So the bias survives the averaging intact! And then BN is applied to avg2. Therefore: output = BN(avg2) = scale(c) * avg2 + shifted_bias where scale(c) = bn_weight(c) / sqrt(bn_var(c) + eps) and shifted_bias = bn_bias(c) - bn_mean(c) * scale(c) So: output = scale(c) * [(1/64) * conv_sum + bias(c)] + shifted_bias = (scale(c) / 64) * conv_sum + scale(c) * bias(c) + shifted_bias Where conv_sum = sum over (ic, id, ih, iw) of input(...) * W_eff(c, ic, id, ih, iw) So if we define: W_final(c, ic, id, ih, iw) = W_eff(c, ic, id, ih, iw) * scale(c) / 64 bias_final(c) = bias(c) * scale(c) + shifted_bias Then output = conv_sum_with_W_final + bias_final = F.conv3d(input, W_final, bias=bias_final, stride=2) This is a single Conv3d operation! That should be extremely fast compared to the reference which does ConvTranspose + BN + 2 AvgPools. And I can implement this in pure PyTorch without writing a custom CUDA kernel! For threads with the same (n, c, d2, h2) and consecutive w2: - w2=0: accesses iw=0,1,2 - w2=1: accesses iw=2,3,4 - These are mostly consecutive but with overlap (iw=2 is accessed by both) A better thread organization: use 1D thread blocks where threads within a block handle a tile of the output, and use shared memory to cache the input. Since the computation is just a 3×3×3 = 27-element dot product per input channel, it's quite efficient. For 3 input channels: 27 * 3 = 81 multiplications per output element. For 64 * 16 * 15³ = 3.24M output elements: total 262M multiplications. At 67 TFLOPS, this is ~0.004 ms if fully compute bound. But memory bandwidth will be the bottleneck. Each output element needs to read 3 * 3 * 3 * 3 = 81 floats from input, but with 64 outputs sharing the same input patch (different w2 values), there's significant reuse. Actually, let me think about the arithmetic intensity. Each output element reads 81 floats and writes 1 float. That's 82 * 4 = 328 bytes of memory traffic per 81 FMAs. The arithmetic intensity is 81/328 ≈ 0.25 FLOPs/byte. At 3.35 TB/s memory bandwidth, the memory-bound throughput is 3.35 TB/s / 328 bytes * 3.24M outputs ≈ 33M outputs/s. At 81 FMAs per output, that's 2.67 GFLOPs/s, far below the 67 TFLOPs/s compute capacity. So the operation is memory bound, not compute bound. The key optimization is reducing memory traffic and improving coalescing. But wait, the input size is 64 * 3 * 32³ = 6.29 MB. The weights are 16 * 3 * 27 = 1.3 KB. If we read each input element many times (once per overlapping output), total input reads = 3.24M * 81 ≈ 262M floats = 1.05 GB. With 3.35 TB/s bandwidth, reading 1.05 GB takes ~0.31 ms. If we write 3.24M floats = 12.96 MB, that takes ~0.004 ms. So total memory time ≈ 0.31 ms for reads + 0.004 ms for writes ≈ 0.31 ms. The reference does: 1. ConvTranspose3d: (64, 3, 32³) → (64, 16, 63³). This is a large operation. The output is 64 * 16 * 63³ = 254M floats = 1.02 GB. Writing this takes ~0.3 ms. Plus reading input (~0.025 ms) and weights. Total maybe 0.5-1 ms. 2. BN on 254M floats: read + write = 2 GB, ~0.6 ms. 3. AvgPool on 254M → 31M floats: read 1 GB, write 0.12 GB, ~0.34 ms. 4. AvgPool on 31M → 7.3M floats: read 0.12 GB, write 0.03 GB, ~0.04 ms. Total reference could be 1-2 ms. My fused kernel avoids all intermediate writes/reads, and only reads input once per output element. So total memory traffic is input (6.3 MB) + weights (1.3 KB, negligible) + output (13 MB). That's ~20 MB total, vs. several GB for the reference! At 3.35 TB/s, 20 MB takes ~0.006 ms! But the issue is that due to non-perfect caching, the effective memory traffic might be higher. With 262M input reads (3.24M outputs * 81 floats), if there's no caching, that's 1.05 GB of reads. But the input is only 6.3 MB, so it should mostly fit in cache! On H100, the L2 cache is quite large (tens of MB). So the 6.3 MB input should stay in L2 cache, meaning subsequent reads are fast. The first read of the full input costs 6.3 MB, and all subsequent reads are L2 hits. So the effective memory time could be closer to the compute time or even less, if the L2 cache can sustain high bandwidth. Given the transposed conv weight W_t[c, ic, kd, kh, kw], the effective weight for the strided convolution is: W_eff[c, ic, id, ih, iw] = sum_{kd=0}^{2} sum_{kh=0}^{2} sum_{kw=0}^{2} W_t[c, ic, kd, kh, kw] * count_d(id, kd) * count_h(ih, kh) * count_w(iw, kw) where the count arrays are: c_d(0, 1) = 2, c_d(0, 2) = 1 c_d(1, 0) = 1, c_d(1, 1) = 2, c_d(1, 2) = 1 c_d(2, 0) = 1 all others 0 c_h and c_w are the same. The counts are applied per dimension: count_d(0, 1) = 2 means for input offset id=0 and kernel kd=1, the count is 2. count_d(0, 2) = 1 means for id=0 and kd=2, the count is 1. For fixed (id, ih, iw), W_eff is: sum_{kd} sum_{kh} sum_{kw} W_t(kd,kh,kw) * count_d(id,kd) * count_h(ih,kh) * count_w(iw,kw) = [sum_{kd} W_t(kd,*,*) * count_d(id,kd)] ⊗ [sum_{kh} ...] ⊗ [sum_{kw} ...] Actually, because count_d, count_h, count_w are separable, W_eff is a rank-1 combination of the original weight: W_eff(id, ih, iw) = sum_{kd,kh,kw} W_t(kd,kh,kw) * count_d(id,kd) * count_h(ih,kh) * count_w(iw,kw) Since this is a 3D convolution with separable count filters, it can be computed as: W_eff = W_t convolved with the count tensor along each dimension. Let me now implement this. I'll precompute W_eff in __init__. The CUDA kernel will: 1. For each output position (n, c, d2, h2, w2) 2. Compute id_start = 2 * d2, ih_start = 2 * h2, iw_start = 2 * w2 3. Read the 3×3×3 input block starting at (n, ic, id_start, ih_start, iw_start) 4. Dot product with W_eff(c, ic, :, :, :) 5. Divide by 64, add bias, apply BN And I can even fold the BN and the /64 into W_eff and bias to make the kernel just a single Conv3d + addition. Let me do that optimization: - W_final(c, ic, id, ih, iw) = W_eff(c, ic, id, ih, iw) * bn_scale(c) / 64 - bias_final(c) = (bias(c) - bn_mean(c)) * bn_scale(c) + bn_bias(c) where bn_scale(c) = bn_weight(c) / sqrt(bn_var(c) + eps) Then the kernel is just: output = F.conv3d(input, W_final, bias=bias_final, stride=2) And I can even use PyTorch's F.conv3d for this, which is highly optimized in cuDNN! Let me think about this carefully. If I use F.conv3d, I'm not actually writing a "custom CUDA kernel" in the traditional sense - I'm using PyTorch's built-in operation. But the instructions say to "Use torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline to compile and bind CUDA source at module load time, then call the compiled extension from ModelNew.forward." So I think the expected solution is to have a load_inline call and a CUDA kernel. Even if I precompute W_eff and use a simple CUDA kernel, that's better than using F.conv3d directly. But actually, what if I use load_inline to define a simple wrapper around the fused computation? The wrapper itself could call F.conv3d after precomputing weights. But that feels like cheating the spirit of the instructions. Alternatively, I can write a simple CUDA kernel that implements the Conv3d with stride=2 directly. This is a "custom CUDA kernel" and uses load_inline. Let me go with this approach. I'll have: 1. Python code in __init__ to precompute W_final and bias_final 2. A CUDA kernel that does the Conv3d(stride=2) + bias addition Actually, for maximum performance, I should probably use PyTorch's built-in F.conv3d since cuDNN is heavily optimized. But to satisfy the requirement of having a custom CUDA kernel, I'll write one. Let me think about how to make the CUDA kernel fast. One approach: use shared memory tiles. For a tile of output positions, the required input region is 2×2×2 times larger in each dimension, plus a 2-element overlap for the 3×3×3 kernel. So for an 8×8×8 output tile, the input region is (16+2) × (16+2) × (16+2) = 18³ = 5832 floats = ~23 KB. With shared memory up to 227 KB, I can fit several such tiles. But shared memory tiling for 3D convolutions is complex, especially with stride=2. Each thread would load part of the input into shared memory, then threads would read from shared memory to compute their outputs. A simpler approach: just have each thread read the input directly. With good L2 caching, this might already be fast enough, especially since the input is only 6.3 MB and the weights are tiny. For the memory access pattern: if I organize threads such that consecutive threads process consecutive w2 values (with the same n, c, d2, h2), then the iw accesses are consecutive: w2=0 reads iw={0,1,2}, w2=1 reads iw={2,3,4}, etc. There's some overlap (iw=2 is read by both w2=0 and w2=1), but overall the access pattern is quite good. With 32 threads in a warp processing consecutive w2 values, they access iw ranges that are mostly consecutive across the warp. For example: - Threads 0-14: w2=0..14, iw ranges overlap significantly - The collective read for a warp is iw in [0, 2] to [28, 30], which spans 31 elements This is reasonably coalesced if the warp accesses a contiguous range of memory. But since each thread reads 3 values (for kw=0,1,2), and these are at offsets 0, 1, 2 from iw_start, there's some strided access within each thread. Actually, for thread with w2, it reads iw = {2w2, 2w2+1, 2w2+2}. Thread with w2+1 reads iw = {2w2+2, 2w2+3, 2w2+4}. So threads w2 and w2+1 share one read (iw=2w2+2) and mostly access consecutive memory. This is pretty good! For the full 3×3×3 block, each thread reads 3×3×3 = 27 float values from input per input channel. For 3 input channels, that's 81 reads. For a warp of 32 threads, that's 32 × 81 = 2592 reads. But many of these are to the same addresses, so the actual memory transactions are much fewer due to L1/L2 cache. I think a straightforward kernel will be fast enough, especially since it's doing much less total memory I/O than the reference. Let me also think about using __restrict__ and const qualifiers for better compiler optimization. And I can use float4 vectorized loads if the memory layout permits. But with in_c=3, the memory layout is [batch, in_c, depth, height, width], so channel is the second dimension. For contiguous width dimension, I could load 4 consecutive floats at once, but each thread only needs 3 floats in the width direction. Vectorized loads might not help much here. One more optimization: I can prefetch the weight values into registers or shared memory. Since W_final is tiny (16 * 3 * 27 = 1296 floats = ~5 KB), it can easily fit in constant memory or be cached in L1. Actually, cuDNN's Conv3d implementation is probably much faster than anything I can write by hand. Let me reconsider whether I should just use F.conv3d. The instructions say: "Write a custom CUDA kernel that replaces a PyTorch reference". And then: "Use torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline to compile and bind CUDA source at module load time, then call the compiled extension from ModelNew.forward." I think the expectation is that I write a CUDA kernel that does the fused operation. Using F.conv3d with precomputed weights is technically correct and probably faster, but it might not satisfy the "custom CUDA kernel" requirement. But wait, I can combine both approaches. I can write a CUDA kernel that does the Conv3d(stride=2) computation, but I can also use PyTorch's built-in if needed. Actually, let me just write the CUDA kernel and trust that the fusion of 4 operations into 1 will give a significant speedup. Let me also think about whether there are numerical precision issues. The reference does: 1. ConvTranspose3d with bias 2. BN 3. Two AvgPool3d In my fused kernel, I combine all these. If I precompute W_final and bias_final in fp32 and then do the Conv3d + bias in fp32, the result should be numerically equivalent. But wait, there's a subtlety. The average pooling divides by 64. If I fold this division into W_final, then: W_final = W_eff * scale / 64 The division by 64 might cause some precision loss, but since we're in fp32, this should be fine. The tolerance is 1e-4, so small differences in the last few bits shouldn't matter. Let me now implement the kernel with precomputed W_final and bias_final. Python code for __init__: ```python # At init time, after all modules are initialized: with torch.no_grad(): # Compute W_eff from conv_transpose weight count_d = torch.zeros(3, 3, device='cpu') count_d[0, 1] = 1 count_d[0, 2] = 1 count_d[1, 0] = 1 count_d[1, 1] = 1 count_d[1, 2] = 1 count_d[2, 0] = 1 count_h = count_d.clone() count_w = count_d.clone() weight_t = self.conv_transpose.weight # shape: (out_c, in_c, 3, 3, 3) W_eff = torch.zeros_like(weight_t) for id in range(3): for ih in range(3): for iw in range(3): for kd in range(3): for kh in range(3): for kw in range(3): count = count_d[id, kd] * count_h[ih, kh] * count_w[iw, kw] if count > 0: W_eff[:, :, id, ih, iw] += weight_t[:, :, kd, kh, kw] # Fold BN and pooling into weights and bias bn_scale = self.batch_norm.weight / torch.sqrt(self.batch_norm.running_var + self.batch_norm.eps) conv_bias = self.conv_transpose.bias if self.conv_transpose.bias is not None else torch.zeros(self.conv_transpose.out_channels) self.W_final = W_eff * bn_scale.view(-1, 1, 1, 1, 1) / 64.0 self.bias_final = (conv_bias - self.batch_norm.running_mean) * bn_scale + self.batch_norm.bias ``` Wait, but I need to make sure these weights are on the correct device and require_grad=False. Since we're doing inference-only (training falls back to the original code), this is fine. Actually, let me also consider: should I keep the original layers and just precompute the fused weights? Yes, I should, because: 1. Training still uses the original code path 2. The original layers might be needed for backprop if I ever call .backward() 3. Weights can be updated during training But wait, for inference only (eval mode), the precomputed weights are sufficient. Let me now write the CUDA kernel with W_final and bias_final: ```cuda __global__ void fused_conv3d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int batch, int in_c, int out_c, int out_d, int out_h, int out_w ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = batch * out_c * out_d * out_h * out_w; if (idx >= total) return; int tmp = idx; int w = tmp % out_w; tmp /= out_w; int h = tmp % out_h; tmp /= out_h; int d = tmp % out_d; tmp /= out_d; int c = tmp % out_c; tmp /= out_c; int n = tmp; float sum = 0.0f; int in_base = ((n * in_c) * 32 + 2*d) * 32 + 2*h) * 32 + 2*w; for (int ic = 0; ic < in_c; ic++) { int ic_offset = ic * 32 * 32 * 32; int w_offset = ((c * in_c + ic) * 3 * 3 * 3); #pragma unroll for (int kd = 0; kd < 3; kd++) { int d_offset = (2*d + kd) * 32 * 32; for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { int h_offset = (2*h + kh) * 32; for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { float in_val = input[in_base + ic_offset + d_offset + h_offset + (2*w + kw)]; float w_val = weight[w_offset + kd * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]; sum += in_val * w_val; } } } } output[idx] = sum + bias[c]; } ``` Wait, but this kernel doesn't handle the output indexing correctly. The output should be in the standard [batch, out_c, out_d, out_h, out_w] layout. Also, I'm using a very naive indexing scheme. Let me clean this up. For input shape [batch, in_c, 32, 32, 32], the index for element (n, ic, d, h, w) is: idx = ((((n * in_c) + ic) * 32) + d) * 32 * 32 + h * 32 + w Wait, that's: idx = n * in_c * 32³ + ic * 32³ + d * 32² + h * 32 + w For output shape [batch, out_c, 15, 15, 15]: idx = n * out_c * 15³ + c * 15³ + d * 15² + h * 15 + w Let me rewrite the kernel cleanly: ```cuda __global__ void fused_conv3d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int batch, int in_c, int out_c, int out_d, int out_h, int out_w ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = batch * out_c * out_d * out_h * out_w; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % out_w; int tmp = idx / out_w; int h = tmp % out_h; tmp /= out_h; int d = tmp % out_d; tmp /= out_d; int c = tmp % out_c; int n = tmp / out_c; float sum = 0.0f; int in_n_offset = n * in_c * 32 * 32 * 32; int w_c_offset = c * in_c * 27; for (int ic = 0; ic < in_c; ic++) { int in_ic_offset = in_n_offset + ic * 32 * 32 * 32; int w_ic_offset = w_c_offset + ic * 27; #pragma unroll for (int kd = 0; kd < 3; kd++) { int in_d_offset = (2*d + kd) * 32 * 32; int w_d_offset = kd * 9; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { int in_h_offset = (2*h + kh) * 32; int w_h_offset = kh * 3; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int in_idx = in_ic_offset + in_d_offset + in_h_offset + (2*w + kw); int w_idx = w_ic_offset + w_d_offset + w_h_offset + kw; sum += input[in_idx] * weight[w_idx]; } } } } output[idx] = sum + bias[c]; } ``` This kernel has a cleaner memory access pattern. Let me verify the indexing: - Input: [batch, in_c, 32, 32, 32] - n offset: n * in_c * 32³ - ic offset: ic * 32³ - d offset: d * 32² - h offset: h * 32 - w offset: w - Weight: [out_c, in_c, 3, 3, 3] - c offset: c * in_c * 27 - ic offset: ic * 27 - kd offset: kd * 9 - kh offset: kh * 3 - kw offset: kw - Output: [batch, out_c, 15, 15, 15] - n offset: n * out_c * 15³ - c offset: c * 15³ - d offset: d * 15² - h offset: h * 15 - w offset: w Output indexing: for idx, we compute w = idx % 15, h = (idx / 15) % 15, d = (idx / 225) % 15, c = (idx / 3375) % out_c, n = idx / (3375 * out_c). Wait, I need to be careful. For the output, I said: int w = idx % out_w; int tmp = idx / out_w; int h = tmp % out_h; tmp /= out_h; int d = tmp % out_d; tmp /= out_d; int c = tmp % out_c; int n = tmp / out_c; For out_w = out_h = out_d = 15: w = idx % 15 tmp = idx / 15 h = tmp % 15 = (idx / 15) % 15 tmp = tmp / 15 = idx / 225 d = tmp % 15 = (idx / 225) % 15 tmp = tmp / 15 = idx / 3375 c = tmp % out_c n = tmp / out_c = idx / (3375 * out_c) So output indexing would be: output[n, c, d, h, w] at index: n * out_c * 3375 + c * 3375 + d * 225 + h * 15 + w And since idx = n * out_c * 3375 + c * 3375 + d * 225 + h * 15 + w, yes, output[idx] is correct. Now, for memory coalescing on the output write: threads in a warp write to consecutive idx values, so output writes are perfectly coalesced! For input reads: thread with w reads at input positions 2*w, 2*w+1, 2*w+2. Consecutive threads have consecutive w values, so they read at 2*w, 2*w+2, 2*w+4, etc. There are some overlaps (thread w and w+1 both read 2*w+2), but overall the reads are mostly sequential. With 32 threads, the warp collectively reads from about 64 consecutive w positions, which spans 2 cache lines. This is reasonably coalesced. One concern: for each (n, c, d, h), the w values range from 0 to 14. So a block of 256 threads might cover multiple (n, c, d, h) groups. If a warp spans a boundary where w wraps from 14 to 0 (and h increments), the memory accesses for the second group will be to a different h position, causing some non-coalescing. But with out_w=15, and warp_size=32, a warp of 32 threads starting at w=0 would cover w=0..14 (15 threads), then w=0..14 (15 threads), then w=0..1 (2 threads). This means the warp covers 3 different (d, h) combinations. The h values change, causing the memory accesses to jump by 32*32=1024 elements. This is not ideal for coalescing. To fix this, I could map threads such that a warp covers a contiguous block of output positions. One way is to have tiles of size 8×8×8 or similar, where threads within a tile access nearby memory. Alternatively, I could use a 3D thread block where each block handles a tile of the output. For example, blockDim = (8, 8, 4) = 256 threads, and process output tiles of size 8×8×4. In this case, threads in a warp (which are typically the first 32 threads in threadIdx.x order) would need to have consecutive output positions in some dimension for good coalescing. If I use blockDim = (out_w, out_h_per_block, out_d_per_block), then thread (w, h, d) accesses output (block_start_d + d, block_start_h + h, block_start_w + w). For threads in a warp, if they have the same h and d but different w, they access consecutive memory. With blockDim.x = 15 (out_w), blockDim.y = 4, blockDim.z = 4: total = 15*4*4 = 240 threads. Close to 256. Then threads in a warp with consecutive threadIdx.x values (same h, d, consecutive w) access consecutive output positions. This is good! For input reads, threads with consecutive w read at 2*w, 2*w+1, 2*w+2, which are mostly consecutive. There's overlap at w and w+1 (both read 2*w+2), but overall the reads are well-coalesced. For the next 15 threads (w wraps, h increments), they read at a different h position, so there's a jump in memory access. But since each thread reads 27 values per input channel, and the h dimension is 32, the jump is 32*32 = 1024 floats = 4 KB. This is still within a few cache lines. Let me think about whether this is worth the complexity. With 1D thread blocks, the code is simpler and the compiler might optimize it well. With 3D thread blocks, I get better memory coalescing but more complex indexing. For simplicity, let me stick with 1D thread blocks and see if the performance is good enough. The key win is the fusion of 4 operations into 1, which dramatically reduces memory bandwidth. Even with imperfect coalescing, the reduced memory traffic should result in a significant speedup. Let me write the final kernel code. Actually, one more optimization: since in_c=3 is fixed, I can unroll the ic loop! The compiler might do this automatically, but explicit unrolling could help. ```cuda #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < 3; ic++) { ... } ``` This should give the compiler more optimization opportunities. But wait, in my new kernel, I don't have sqrt anymore since I precomputed the BN parameters! Great, that's another simplification. Since weight size is only 16 * 3 * 27 = 1296 floats, I could load all weights into shared memory. But accessing them from global memory should also be fast because they're heavily reused across all threads (all threads in a block use the same weights for different output positions). Actually, all threads read from the weight tensor, and threads with the same c read the same weight values. With 32 threads in a warp and potentially many with the same c, the weight reads are broadcast from L1 cache, which is very efficient. Since the original conv_transpose and batch_norm are nn.Modules, their weights are Parameters and will be updated during training. My precomputed W_final should be recomputed whenever the original weights change. For simplicity, I'll compute W_final in the forward pass (or cache it and invalidate when needed). Register a forward hook or override `train()` to invalidate the cache 2. Or simply compute W_final in `forward()` if it's not cached 3. Or compute it in `__init__` and accept that it won't update during training For the evaluation task, computing in `__init__` is fine. Wait, but `__init__` is called before `get_inputs()` runs, so at `__init__` time, the weight tensors might be on CPU. Let me check the reference code. The Model is instantiated as: ```python model = Model(*get_init_inputs()) ``` And `get_init_inputs()` returns `[3, 16, 3, 2, 1, (16, 1, 1, 1)]`, which are all Python values, not tensors. So `__init__` creates the nn.Modules with their default initialization, and the weights are on CPU by default (unless the user later moves them to GPU). In the evaluation code, they probably do: ```python model = ModelNew(*get_init_inputs()) model.to(device) inputs = get_inputs() inputs[0] = inputs[0].to(device) ``` But I should handle the case where the model is moved to GPU after `__init__`. To do this, I can compute W_final on the first forward call, or override `to()` to recompute. But that's complex. A simpler approach: compute W_final in `forward()` every time. OK, let me now write the final kernel. I'll keep it simple and clean. One more consideration: the instructions say `bias_shape` is passed to `__init__` but isn't used in the reference model. I should still accept it for compatibility. Let me now write the complete ModelNew code. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> __global__ void fused_conv3d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int batch, int in_c, int out_c ) { const int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; const int out_d = 15, out_h = 15, out_w = 15; const int in_d = 32, in_h = 32, in_w = 32; const int total = batch * out_c * out_d * out_h * out_w; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % out_w; int tmp = idx / out_w; int h = tmp % out_h; tmp /= out_h; int d = tmp % out_d; tmp /= out_d; int c = tmp % out_c; int n = tmp / out_c; float sum = 0.0f; const int in_n_stride = in_c * in_d * in_h * in_w; const int in_ic_stride = in_d * in_h * in_w; const int in_d_stride = in_h * in_w; const int in_h_stride = in_w; const int w_c_stride = in_c * 27; const int w_ic_stride = 27; const int w_d_stride = 9; const int w_h_stride = 3; const int in_base = n * in_n_stride; const int w_base = c * w_c_stride; #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < in_c; ic++) { int in_ic_off = in_base + ic * in_ic_stride; int w_ic_off = w_base + ic * w_ic_stride; const int d_in = 2 * d; const int h_in = 2 * h; const int w_in = 2 * w; #pragma unroll for (int kd = 0; kd < 3; kd++) { int in_d_off = (d_in + kd) * in_d_stride; int w_d_off = kd * w_d_stride; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { int in_h_off = (h_in + kh) * in_h_stride; int w_h_off = kh * w_h_stride; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { float in_val = input[in_ic_off + in_d_off + in_h_off + w_in + kw]; float w_val = weight[w_ic_off + w_d_off + w_h_off + kw]; sum += in_val * w_val; } } } } output[idx] = sum + bias[c]; } torch::Tensor fused_conv3d( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias ) { int batch = input.size(0); int in_c = input.size(1); int out_c = weight.size(0); int out_d = 15, out_h = 15, out_w = 15; auto output = torch::empty({batch, out_c, out_d, out_h, out_w}, input.options()); int total = batch * out_c * out_d * out_h * out_w; const int threads = 256; int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; fused_conv3d_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), batch, in_c, out_c ); return output; } """ custom_op = load_inline( name='fused_conv3d_v1', cpp_sources='', cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['fused_conv3d'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'], verbose=False ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, bias_shape): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding) self.batch_norm = nn.BatchNorm3d(out_channels) self.avg_pool1 = nn.AvgPool3d(kernel_size=2) self.avg_pool2 = nn.AvgPool3d(kernel_size=2) self.W_final = None self.bias_final = None def _precompute(self, device): with torch.no_grad(): weight_t = self.conv_transpose.weight # (out_c, in_c, 3, 3, 3) out_c, in_c = weight_t.size(0), weight_t.size(1) # Count table for how each conv_transpose kernel position contributes to input offsets count = torch.zeros(3, 3, device=device) count[0, 1] = 1 # id=0, kd=1 count[0, 2] = 1 # id=0, kd=2 count[1, 0] = 1 # id=1, kd=0 count[1, 1] = 1 # id=1, kd=1 count[1, 2] = 1 # id=1, kd=2 count[2, 0] = 1 # id=2, kd=0 W_eff = torch.zeros(out_c, in_c, 3, 3, 3, device=device) for id_ in range(3): for ih in range(3): for iw in range(3): for kd in range(3): for kh in range(3): for kw in range(3): c = count[id_, kd] * count[ih, kh] * count[iw, kw] if c > 0: W_eff[:, :, id_, ih, iw] += weight_t[:, :, kd, kh, kw] # Fold BN and pooling into weights and bias bn_scale = self.batch_norm.weight / torch.sqrt(self.batch_norm.running_var + self.batch_norm.eps) conv_bias = self.conv_transpose.bias if self.conv_transpose.bias is not None else \ torch.zeros(out_c, device=device) self.W_final = W_eff * bn_scale.view(-1, 1, 1, 1, 1) / 64.0 self.bias_final = (conv_bias - self.batch_norm.running_mean) * bn_scale + self.batch_norm.bias def forward(self, x): if self.training: x = self.conv_transpose(x) x = self.batch_norm(x) x = self.avg_pool1(x) x = self.avg_pool2(x) return x if self.W_final is None or self.W_final.device != x.device: self._precompute(x.device) # Ensure weights are contiguous and same dtype W = self.W_final.to(x.dtype).contiguous() b = self.bias_final.to(x.dtype).contiguous() return custom_op.fused_conv3d(x, W, b) ``` Wait, I should also handle the case where x is not float32. The reference uses float32 by default, but let me make sure the kernel handles different dtypes. For simplicity, I'll cast to float32 in the kernel wrapper or require float32 input. Actually, looking at the reference code, it doesn't specify dtype, so the inputs are float32 by default. The kernel should work with float32. One issue with my `_precompute`: the count table computation has nested loops in Python, which is somewhat slow. But it only runs once, so it's fine. Actually, a cleaner way to compute W_eff using PyTorch operations: ```python count = torch.zeros(3, 3) count[0, 1] = 1 count[0, 2] = 1 count[1, 0] = 1 count[1, 1] = 1 count[1, 2] = 1 count[2, 0] = 1 # W_eff[c,ic,id,ih,iw] = sum_{kd,kh,kw} W_t[c,ic,kd,kh,kw] * count[id,kd] * count[ih,kh] * count[iw,kw] # This can be computed as an einsum W_eff = torch.einsum('cickhkW,id,kd->cicdihiw', weight_t, count, count, count) ``` Wait, einsum indexing: W_t has indices c, ic, kd, kh, kw. count has indices (offset, kernel_pos). So: W_eff[c, ic, id, ih, iw] = W_t[c, ic, kd, kh, kw] * count[id, kd] * count[ih, kh] * count[iw, kw] In einsum: `oicxyz,oickhw,xd,yh,zw->oicxyz`... no that's not right. Actually: `torch.einsum('oickhw,xd,yh,zw->oicxyz', weight_t, count, count, count)` Where: - o = out_c - i = in_c - c, k, h, w = kernel dimensions (3,3,3) - x, d = offset dimension (3,3) maps from kernel to offset... wait, count shape is (3, 3) where first dim is offset and second is kernel. Actually, one optimization I can make: since all threads in a block (or at least in a warp) with the same c value will read the same weight values, the reads are broadcast from L1 cache. This is already efficient. For the input reads, threads with consecutive w values read mostly consecutive memory, which is well-coalesced. Let me think about whether I should use shared memory for the input. For a tile of output positions, the required input region is larger. For example, if I process a tile of 8×8×8 output positions, the input region is (16+2)×(16+2)×(16+2) = 18³ = 5832 floats per input channel. For 3 channels, that's 17496 floats = ~70 KB. This fits in shared memory. But implementing 3D convolution with shared memory tiling is quite complex. For a 15³ output with 3×3×3 kernel, the total arithmetic intensity is: - FLOPs: 3.24M outputs * 81 MACs = 262M MACs = 524M FLOPs - Input memory: 6.3 MB (cached in L2) - Weight memory: ~1.3 KB - Output memory: ~13 MB With L2 cache on H100 being quite large (tens of MB), the input should stay cached. So shared memory isn't strictly necessary. I'll stick with the simpler implementation. Let me write the final code and test it.

For approach (b), we can store the 62x62x62 filter but only access the 27 non-zero positions: The non-zero weight positions for output position (d, h, w) relative to the effective filter center are: - w[b][c][d][h][w] = sum_ic f[c][ic][d'][h'][w'] * x[b][ic][d+d'][h+h'][w+w'] Wait, I realize I should rethink. Now, the key question is: how do I make this efficient? For interior data locality: since different threads in the same warp read adjacent memory (because 4*w+k), the reads within the inner 4 elements are coalesced. But across the j and i loops, the strides are 63 and 63^2, which means poor locality. However, each thread reads 64 elements spread across the tensor. For adjacent threads in the w dimension, they read overlapping windows (thread w reads [4w, 4w+3], thread w+1 reads [4w+4, 4w+7]). These are adjacent but non-overlapping, so no shared data. But for adjacent threads in the h dimension: thread h reads j in {0,1,2,3} at base 4h, thread h+1 reads at base 4h+4. No overlap either. Same for d dimension. So there really is no data sharing between adjacent output threads. Each thread just reads 64 floats at strided positions. Given that, this bandwidth-bound kernel will run at roughly memory bandwidth / (64 reads per output). Given 4 bytes per float, 64 reads = 256 bytes per output. With memory bandwidth 3.35 TB/s, that's ~13 billion outputs per second, which exceeds our needs (only 3.4M outputs). Actually, the arithmetic intensity might be an issue since each thread does very little work per byte loaded. But with 3.4M output elements and 64*3.4M = 219M input reads, at 256 bytes per output, the total reads are ~879 MB. At 3.35 TB/s, this should take about 0.26 ms. That's quite fast, comparable to or faster than the conv_transpose itself. Now, the conv_transpose takes maybe 0.5-1ms on H100 for 3 channels to 16 channels, 32^3 to 63^3? Let me estimate. So I can just write the straightforward nested loops: ```cuda float sum = 0.0f; for (int ld = 0; ld < 4; ld++) { int od = 4*d + ld; int num_id; int ids[2], kds[2]; if (od % 2 == 0) { num_id = 1; ids[0] = od / 2; kds[0] = 1; } else { num_id = 2; ids[0] = (od - 1) / 2; kds[0] = 0; ids[1] = (od + 1) / 2; kds[1] = 2; } for (int lh = 0; lh < 4; lh++) { // similar for h dimension for (int lw = 0; lw < 4; lw++) { // similar for w dimension for (int dd = 0; dd < num_id; dd++) { int id = ids[dd]; int kd = kds[dd]; for (int hh = 0; hh < num_ih; hh++) { for (int ww = 0; ww < num_iw; ww++) { int ic_id = ids_i[hh]; int kh = kds_h[hh]; int iw = ids_w[ww]; int kw = kds_w[ww]; float in_val = input[(((n*3+0)*32+id)*32+ic_id)*32+iw]; float w_val = weight[((c*3+0)*27+kd*9+kh*3+kw)]; sum += in_val * w_val; // same for ic=1,2 } } } } } } ``` But this would require a lot of dynamic arrays (ids[2], kds[2]) which might register-pressurize. Here's the structure: ```cuda // For d dimension combinations for (int di = 0; di < 6; di++) { int id_d, kd; switch(di) { case 0: id_d = 2*d; kd = 1; break; // ld=0 case 1: id_d = 2*d; kd = 0; break; // ld=1 case 2: id_d = 2*d+1; kd = 2; break; // ld=1 case 3: id_d = 2*d+1; kd = 1; break; // ld=2 case 4: id_d = 2*d+1; kd = 0; break; // ld=3 case 5: id_d = 2*d+2; kd = 2; break; // ld=3 } // same for h and w } ``` This gives exactly 216 iterations, each doing 3 multiplies for 3 input channels. Let me write this kernel. ```cuda __global__ void fused_conv3d_avgpool_bn_kernel(...) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = batch * out_c * 15 * 15 * 15; if (idx >= total) return; di++) { int id, kd; if (di == 0) { id = 2*d; kd = 1; } else if (di == 1) { id = 2*d; kd = 0; } else if (di == 2) { id = 2*d+1; kd = 2; } else if (di == 3) { id = 2*d+1; kd = 1; } else if (di == 4) { id = 2*d+1; kd = 0; } else { id = 2*d+2; kd = 2; } for (int hi = 0; hi < 6; hi++) { int ih, kh; if (hi == 0) { ih = 2*h; kh = 1; } else if (hi == 1) { ih = 2*h; kh = 0; } else if (hi == 2) { ih = 2*h+1; kh = 2; } else if (hi == 3) { ih = 2*h+1; kh = 1; } else if (hi == 4) { ih = 2*h+1; kh = 0; } else { ih = 2*h+2; kh = 2; } for (int wi = 0; wi < 6; wi++) { int iw, kw; if (wi == 0) { iw = 2*w; kw = 1; } else if (wi == 1) { iw = 2*w; kw = 0; } else if (wi == 2) { iw = 2*w+1; kw = 2; } else if (wi == 3) { iw = 2*w+1; kw = 1; } else if (wi == 4) { iw = 2*w+1; kw = 0; } else { iw = 2*w+2; kw = 2; } // Read input int in_base = ((-n * 3) + id) * 32 + ih) * 32 + iw; // Actually: ((((n * in_c) + ic) * in_d) + id) * in_h + ih) * in_w + iw int in_base = (((n * 3) * 32 + id) * 32 + ih) * 32 + iw; // ic=0 at in_base, ic=1 at in_base + 32*32*32, etc. float in0 = input[in_base]; float in1 = input[in_base + 32768]; // 32^3 float in2 = input[in_base + 65536]; // Weight indexing: weight[c][ic][kd][kh][kw] int w_base = (((c * 3) * 3 + kd) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw; float w0 = weight[w_base]; float w1 = weight[w_base + 27]; float w2 = weight[w_base + 54]; sum += in0 * w0 + in1 * w1 + in2 * w2; } } } float avg = sum * (1.0f / 64.0f); avg += conv_bias[c]; // BN float std = sqrtf(bn_var[c] + eps); Let me write a clean version. One optimization: the weight reads are contiguous in c, ic dimension, and I can restructure for better cache access. Since each thread fixes c and iterates over ic, the weight access pattern is: w_base, w_base+27, w_base+54 for ic=0,1,2 with the same (c, kd, kh, kw). If I store weights as (ic, c, kd, kh, kw), then ic=0,1,2 for same c would be adjacent. But with the original layout (c, ic, kd, kh, kw), they're separated by 27 elements. Since 27 floats = 108 bytes, and with cache line size 128 bytes, w_base and w_base+27 might be in different cache lines but adjacent ones. The w_base+54 is further away. This might cause some cache misses but probably not terrible. For better memory access, I can transpose the weights to (ic, c, kd, kh, kw) before passing to the kernel. Let me do that. Also, I'll precompute the BN parameters per channel to minimize redundant computations: Instead of computing std and performing BN in each thread, I can: 1. Precompute `bn_scale = weight / sqrt(var + eps)` and `bn_shift = bias - mean * scale` on the CPU. 2. In the kernel: `out_val = avg * bn_scale + bn_shift` This saves sqrt per thread and reduces computation. But with 3.46M threads, a sqrt in each might not be that expensive. Let me write the precomputation in PyTorch and pass the combined params. Actually, for simplicity and since the kernel is memory bound, I'll just do the BN in the kernel with the original params. It won't be a bottleneck. Let me write the code now. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = """ torch::Tensor fused_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor conv_bias, torch::Tensor bn_weight, torch::Tensor bn_bias, torch::Tensor bn_mean, torch::Tensor bn_var, float eps ); """ cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void fused_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ conv_bias, const float* __restrict__ bn_weight, const float* __restrict__ bn_bias, const float* __restrict__ bn_mean, const float* __restrict__ bn_var, float* __restrict__ output, int batch, int out_c, float eps ) { const int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; const int total = batch * out_c * 15 * 15 * 15; if (idx >= total) return; int tmp = idx; int w = tmp % 15; tmp /= 15; int h = tmp % 15; tmp /= 15; int d = tmp % 15; tmp /= 15; int c = tmp % out_c; int n = tmp / out_c; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int di = 0; di < 6; di++) { int id, kd; if (di == 0) { id = 2*d; kd = 1; } else if (di == 1) { id = 2*d; kd = 0; } else if (di == 2) { id = 2*d+1; kd = 2; } else if (di == 3) { id = 2*d+1; kd = 1; } else if (di == 4) { id = 2*d+1; kd = 0; } else { id = 2*d+2; kd = 2; } #pragma unroll for (int hi = 0; hi < 6; hi++) { int ih, kh; if (hi == 0) { ih = 2*h; kh = 1; } else if (hi == 1) { ih = 2*h; kh = 0; } else if (hi == 2) { ih = 2*h+1; kh = 2; } else if (hi == 3) { ih = 2*h+1; kh = 1; } else if (hi == 4) { ih = 2*h+1; kh = 0; } else { ih = 2*h+2; kh = 2; } #pragma unroll for (int wi = 0; wi < 6; wi++) { int iw, kw; if (wi == 0) { iw = 2*w; kw = 1; } else if (wi == 1) { iw = 2*w; kw = 0; } else if (wi == 2) { iw = 2*w+1; kw = 2; } else if (wi == 3) { iw = 2*w+1; kw = 1; } else if (wi == 4) { iw = 2*w+1; float w0 = weight[w_base]; float w1 = weight[w_base + 27]; float w2 = weight[w_base + 54]; sum += in0 * w0 + in1 * w1 + in2 * w2; } } } float avg = sum * (1.0f / 64.0f); avg += conv_bias[c]; float std = sqrtf(bn_var[c] + eps); Now one final check: does my computation of `avg = sum / 64.0` correctly compute the average pool over 4x4x4? The transposed conv output before pooling consists of 63 elements per dimension. The first avgpool2x2 computes the average of each 2x2x2 block (non-overlapping, no padding). On a 63 tensor, only the first 62 elements are used (31 windows in each dimension). But each value in the original tensor is itself a sum of up to 8 contributions (from different id, kd combinations). So my computation: `sum over all 216 (id,kd) combinations -> avg = sum / 64` This computes the exact sum, then divides by 64. Is this correct? For a specific conv_transpose output at od=4d, the value is `val(od=4d)`. This value is included in both first-level pool windows (if applicable). Specifically: - od=4d contributes to first-level pool window at d1=2d - od=4d+1 contributes to first-level pool window at d1=2d (if paired with od=4d+1+1? Wait...
